Ninth Preference: Custom and Ease
Explains preference based on custom and ease among equal statements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the ninth preference rule to resolve conflicting equal-level statements in Hanafi fatwa.

Core Idea
Among equal statements, choose the one aligned with custom and ease.

When two statements within the Hanafi school carry equal authority, the mufti must decide which to rely on. The ninth preference rule provides a clear criterion: prefer the statement that conforms to the custom of the time and brings ease to the people. This lesson explains the condition, the rationale, and the limits of this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for application
The rule applies only when two statements are of equal level. Equal level means both have the same authority in the Hanafi hierarchy (e.g., both from Zahir al-Riwayah with equal correction status).
2
Define 'equal level' in Hanafi rasm al-mufti
Understand the hierarchy: statements may be both mufta bihi or both non-mufta bihi. If one is stronger, this rule does not apply.
3
Apply the preference criteria
Choose the statement that aligns with prevailing custom (ʿurf) and brings ease (suhūla). This reflects the Hanafi principle of rafʿ al-ḥaraj.
4
Recognize the rationale
The Shariʿah aims to facilitate, not burden. Custom indicates what is practical and accepted.
5
Note the limits
This rule does not override a stronger dalīl or a majority opinion. It only resolves ties among equals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نویں وجہ ترجیح: متعد د مساوی درجے کے اقوال میں سے کسی کا عرف و عادت کے موافق ہونا  دو قول مساوی درجے کے ہوں،( دونوں کی تصحیح وارد ہوئی ہو یا دونوں میں سے کسی کی تصیح نہ کی گئی ہو ) تو ایسی صورت میں جو قول اہل زمانہ کے عرف و عادت کے موافق ہوگا، جس میں لوگوں کے لیے آسانی ہوگی ، اس پر فتوی دیا جائے گا؟ کیوں کہ یہ سب چیزیں اُس قول کے لیے باعث ترجیح ہوں گی۔
Ninth reason for preference: among multiple equal-level statements, one being in accordance with custom and habit. Two statements of equal level (both have been corrected or neither has been corrected), then in such a case, whichever statement conforms to the custom and habit of the people of the time, which brings ease for the people, fatwa will be given on that? Because all these things will be a cause of preference for that statement.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This rule addresses situations where two statements are equal in authority—both have been corrected or both are uncorrected. In such a tie, the mufti should prefer the statement that aligns with the prevailing custom (ʿurf) and habit of the people, especially if it brings ease (suhūla). This reflects the Hanafi principle that custom can determine the application of law, and that removing hardship is a key objective. The rule is practical for contemporary fatwa.

Key Terms
عرف (ʿurf): Customary practice of a community that is not opposed to Sharīʿah.
عادت (ʿādah): Habit or recurring practice, similar to custom; often synonymous with ʿurf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When two statements are of equal level (both corrected or both uncorrected), which one should be preferred according to the ninth reason?
A) The one that is more strictly based on original texts.
B) The one that conforms to custom and brings ease.
C) The one that is older in the school.
D) The one that is more favorable to the rich.

Key Takeaway
When two Hanafi statements are of equal authority, the mufti should prefer the one that conforms to custom and brings ease.

The Tenth Preference: Stronger Proof Decides
States preference for stronger proof among corrected statements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the tenth preference rule when two corrected statements exist: identify which statement has stronger proof and state the mufti's duty.

Core Idea
The mufti prefers the opinion with stronger evidence, even if both are authenticated.

When two conflicting statements within the Hanafi school are both corrected (authentic), the mufti cannot simply choose arbitrarily. This tenth rule guides him: he must examine the underlying proof (dalīl) of each statement and give fatwa based on the one with stronger evidence. This ensures that fatwa remains grounded in the School's evidentiary hierarchy.

Guided Path
1
Identify two corrected statements
Confirm both statements are 'corrected' (musahhah) within the school, meaning they have been adopted as valid positions by authoritative Hanafi scholars.
Corrected does not mean equal in strength; only that both are acceptable narrations.
2
Retrieve the dalīl for each statement
For each statement, find the primary evidence (Qur'an, Sunnah, etc.) that the school's scholars used to derive that ruling.
Dalīl may include clear Qur'anic text, authentic hadith, or strong qiyās.
3
Compare the strength of the two evidences
Using Hanafi usul criteria, weigh the two dalīls: consider transmission (thubūt) and meaning (dalālah). The stronger proof is preferred.
Strength is not based on personal opinion but on established usul principles.
4
Prefer the statement with stronger proof and give fatwa
Issue the fatwa according to the statement whose dalīl is superior. This is the mufta bihi (operative) position.
If the proofs are equal, the mufti may need further rules (e.g., later texts, consensus).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دسویں وجہ ترجیح بتصیح شدہ مختلف اقوال میں سے کسی کی دلیل کا قوی ہونا  دو قولوں میں سے اگر ہر ایک کی تصحیح کی گئی ہوں، تو جس قول کی دلیل زیادہ مضبوط ہوگی ، اُس پر فتویٰ دیا جائے گا؟ کیوں کہ دلیل کا قوی ہونا، یہ اُس قول کے لیے وجہ ترجیح ہوگی۔
Tenth reason for preference: among corrected different statements, one having a strong proof. If both statements have been corrected, then whichever statement's proof is stronger, fatwa will be given on that? Because the strength of the proof is a reason for preference for that statement.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This is a straightforward rule: when both conflicting statements are corrected (authentic within the school), the mufti must evaluate their underlying evidence (dalīl). The statement supported by the stronger dalīl becomes the preferred opinion for fatwa. This rule reminds that even authentic narrations may vary in evidentiary strength, and the mufti must exercise ijtihād in weighing proofs. This aligns with the hierarchy of sources in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
دلیل (dalīl): Evidence or proof, usually from Qur'an, Sunnah, consensus, or strong analogy.
تصحیح (taṣḥīḥ): The act of verifying a statement as authentic and correct within the school.
فتویٰ (fatwā): A non-binding legal opinion issued by a qualified jurist (mufti).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the tenth reason, when both statements are corrected, what determines preference?
A) The statement that is easier.
B) The statement with stronger proof.
C) The statement preferred by the majority of scholars.
D) The statement based on custom.

Key Takeaway
The mufti must prefer the corrected statement with the stronger evidence, making proof evaluation a core skill in ifta.

When Preferences Apply: The Condition of Taʿāruḍ al-Taṣḥīḥ
Introduces context when the ten preferences become useful

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the ten preferences (tarjīh) apply in Hanafi iftaa and state the condition of taʿāruḍ al-taṣḥīḥ.

Core Idea
The ten preferences apply only when there is contradiction among corrected narrations (taʿāruḍ al-taṣḥīḥ).

Having learned the ten reasons of preference (tarjīh), we now turn to their scope. This passage clarifies that these preferences are not for arbitrary selection but for resolving conflicts among corrected positions. The mufti must first verify a documented contradiction in the tasḥīḥ before applying these rules.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the Context
The passage follows the listing of ten preferences. It now defines their applicability.
2
Identify the Key Phrase
The phrase 'this if tashih is contradictory' signals the condition under which the preferences are useful.
The Arabic reads: 'هذا إذا تعارض التصحيح'
3
Understand the Meaning
The condition is that multiple corrected narrations conflict, i.e., different scholars have authenticated different statements within the madhhab.
For example, one correction says the relied-upon view is X, another says Y.
4
Connect to Mufti's Work
The mufti uses these preferences to choose among conflicting corrected reports, not among original opinions of the Imams.
This ensures consistency in fatwa methodology.
5
Note the Limitation
The text mentions two situations but only one is present; the second is implied (e.g., when there is no contradiction, the preferences are not needed).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
یہ وجوہ ترجیح کب مفید ہوں گی؟  مذکورہ بالا دس وجوہ ترجیح کے بیان کے بعد هذا إذا تـــــــــارض التصحيح .... ا سے علامہ یہی فرمارہے ہیں کہ یہ وجوہ ترجیحات دو صورتوں میں مفید ہوں گی: • جب تصحیح میں تعارض ہو یعنی متعدد قوال کی تصحیح مروی ہو۔
When will these reasons of preference be useful? After the mention of the above ten reasons of preference, 'this if tashih is contradictory...' from Allama, he says that these reasons of preference will be useful in two situations: • When there is contradiction in tashih, i.e., multiple corrections of statements are narrated.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage concludes the section on tarjīḥ by explaining the scope of these ten rules. They are applicable only when there is taʿāruḍ (contradiction) in tasḥīḥ itself—meaning that multiple statements have been corrected by different authorities, creating a conflict in the school's transmitted positions. The text mentions two situations (though only one is present due to truncation): first, when tashih reports conflict. This indicates that the preferences are not for arbitrary choice but for resolving documented disagreements among the correctors. The mufti uses these ten guidelines to determine which corrected version to follow.

Key Terms
تعارض التصحيح (taʿāruḍ al-taṣḥīḥ): Contradiction among corrected narrations, where different statements have been authenticated by different authorities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) When the mufti is issuing a fatwa on a new issue.
D) When there is consensus among scholars.

Key Takeaway
The ten preferences (tarjīḥ) are only operative when there is a documented contradiction among corrected narrations (taʿāruḍ al-taṣḥīḥ).

Ninth and Tenth Tarjih Causes: Custom, Ease, and Proof Strength
Explains two tarjih reasons and when they apply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the ninth and tenth tarjih causes (custom/ease and proof strength) to resolve conflicts between equally verified Hanafi opinions, and identify the two conditions under which these tarjih rules apply.

Core Idea
Custom and ease, or stronger proof, tip the scale when two Hanafi opinions are equally verified.

In the Hanafi mufti's toolkit, not all opinions are equally authoritative. When two verified opinions conflict, the mufti needs rules to prefer one. This lesson introduces two such rules: preferring the opinion that aligns with custom and brings ease, or the one with stronger evidence. But these rules only apply when verification (tasheeh) itself is inconclusive. Let's examine when and how.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two new tarjih causes
The ninth is compatibility with custom ('urf) and ease (yusr). The tenth is strength of proof (quwwat al-dalil). Understand each.
2
Recognize the precondition: equal level or conflict in tasheeh
These causes only operate when opinions are of equal rank, and either both have been verified (tasheeh) but conflict, or neither has been explicitly verified.
They don't apply if one opinion is clearly from a higher authority level (e.g., Zahir al-Riwayah vs. Nawadir).
3
Apply the tarjih cause to a hypothetical conflict
Given two verified opinions, check if one matches current custom and brings ease. If so, prefer it. If not, check which has stronger proof.
4
Understand the broader rasm al-mufti framework
These are the last two of ten tarjih causes. The author explains they are useful only when tasheeh is conflicted or absent. The mufti must use these factors to determine the mufta bihi.
Other factors include being in the matn, being Imam Abu Hanifa's statement, or being from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نویں وجہ ترجیح: متعدد مساوی درجے کے اقوال میں سے کسی کا عرف و عادت کے موافق ہونا ۹۲ دو قول مساوی درجے کے ہوں، دونوں کی تصحیح وارد ہوئی ہو یا دونوں میں سے کسی کی تصیح نہ کی گئی ہو تو ایسی صورت میں جو قول اہل زمانہ کے عرف و عادت کے موافق ہوگا، جس میں لوگوں کے لیے آسانی ہوگی، اس پر فتوی دیا جائے گا؟ کیوں کہ یہ سب چیزیں اُس قول کے لیے باعث ترجیح ہوں گی۔ دسویں وجہ ترجیح بتصیح شدہ مختلف اقوال میں سے کسی کی دلیل کا قوی ہونا دو قولوں میں سے اگر ہر ایک کی تصحیح کی گئی ہوں، تو جس قول کی دلیل زیادہ مضبوط ہوگی، اُس پر فتویٰ دیا جائے گا؟ کیوں کہ دلیل کا قوی ہونا، یہ اُس قول کے لیے وجہ ترجیح ہوگی۔ یہ وجوہ ترجیح کب مفید ہوں گی؟ مذکورہ بالا دس وجوہ ترجیح کے بیان کے بعد هذا إذا تـــــــــارض التصحيح .... ا سے علامہ یہی فرمارہے ہیں کہ یہ وجوہ ترجیحات دو صورتوں میں مفید ہوں گی: • جب تصحیح میں تعارض ہو یعنی متعدد قوال کی تصحیح مروی ہو۔ • اُس وقت جب کسی بھی قول کی صراحۃ کوئی صحیح منقول نہ ہو؛ لہذا جب ایسی صورت پیش آجائے، تو جن وجوہ ترجیح کا علم ابھی آپ کو ہوا، مثلاً اُن میں سے کسی کا قول متون میں ہونا یا امام صاحب صلى الله عليه وسلم کا قول ہونا، یا ظاہر الروایۃ ہونا وغیرہ؛ اِن کے ذریعے ترجیح قائم کر کے فتوی دینے کے عمل کو پورا کیجیے۔
Ninth cause of preference: Among multiple equal-level sayings, one being in accordance with custom and habit. When two sayings are of equal level, either both have been verified (tasheeh) or neither has been verified, then the saying that agrees with the custom and habit of the people of the time, and brings ease to them, is to be given fatwa upon, because all these things will be causes of preference for that saying. Tenth cause of preference: Among different verified sayings, one having a stronger proof. If both sayings have been verified, then the saying whose proof is stronger is to be given fatwa upon, because the strength of the proof is a cause of preference for that saying. When will these causes of preference be useful? After stating the above ten causes, 'this if tasheeh contradicts...' - the author means that these causes of preference will be useful in two situations: (1) When there is conflict in tasheeh, i.e., multiple sayings are transmitted with verification. (2) When there is no explicit transmitted verification for any saying. Therefore, when such a situation occurs, use the causes of preference you have just learned, e.g., one saying being in the matn, or being the statement of Imam Sahib [Abu Hanifa], or being Zahir al-Riwayah, etc., to establish preference and complete the process of giving fatwa.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a rasm al-mufti explanation of two additional reasons (ninth and tenth) for preferring one legal opinion over another when both are at the same level of authority in the Hanafi school. The ninth reason is compatibility with custom ('urf) and ease (yusr). The Hanafi school recognizes that custom changes over time, so a ruling that aligns with contemporary practice and brings ease to people is preferred. The tenth reason is strength of evidence (quwwat al-dalil): if both opinions have been 'verified' (tasheeh), the one with stronger proof is preferred. The passage then clarifies that these tarjih reasons apply only when there is conflict in tasheeh (different verifications) or when no explicit tasheeh exists. It gives examples: a saying being in the matn, being the statement of Imam Abu Hanifa (translation mistakenly says 'Imam Sahib صلى الله عليه وسلم' due to OCR), or being from Zahir al-Riwayah. The mufti should use these factors to determine the preferred opinion.

Key Terms
عرف و عادت (urf wa adah): Custom and habit of the people of that time and place
تصحیح (tasheeh): Verification of a ruling's authenticity and reliance in the school
ظاہر الروایۃ (Zahir al-Riwayah): The six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani containing authoritative rulings

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When can a mufti use custom and ease to prefer one opinion over another?
A) Only when both opinions are from different schools
B) When both opinions are of equal level and no clear tasheeh or conflict in tasheeh exists
C) Only when the opinion is from a later scholar
D) Never; custom cannot override textual authority

Key Takeaway
Custom, ease, and proof strength are valid tarjih factors only after authority levels have been exhausted and when verification is conflicting or absent.

Rule on Acting upon Mafhum Mukhalif
Couplet and commentary on the mufti's use of mafhum mukhalif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the later Hanafi rule on mafhum mukhalif to determine whether a fatwa can be based on implied meaning, and identify when explicit texts override.

Core Idea
The mufti may act upon mafhum mukhalif unless a clear, established text contradicts it.

This lesson explains a key rasm al-mufti rule from Ibn 'Abidin's commentary on the poem: the mufti can derive rulings from the opposite implication (mafhum mukhalif) of transmitted reports, rational proofs, and Companions' analogizable statements, as long as no explicit text opposes. Understanding this rule helps in weighing implicit versus explicit evidence in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the rule
The couplet commands: 'Act upon mafhum mukhalif of reports that come, as long as it does not contradict a clear, established [text].' Take note of the condition.
The alif in 'thabata' is for prolongation as per the commentary.
2
Identify the scope
According to the commentary, the rule applies to four categories: transmitted fiqh narrations, rational proofs, everyday speech, and ijtihadi statements of Companions that are mudrak bil-qiyas (derivable by analogy).
Companion statements not derivable by qiyas are treated like marfu' hadith.
3
Recognize the exception
If a Companion's statement is not mudrak bil-qiyas, it is treated like marfu' hadith, where mafhum mukhalif is not considered.
This preserves the special status of non-analogizable Companion statements.
4
Apply the condition
If you find an explicit text (sarih) that contradicts the mafhum mukhalif, the explicit text prevails; do not act on the implied meaning.
The explicit text is 'ra'ij' (current) over the implied.
5
Conclude
Thus, the mufti uses mafhum mukhalif as a valid source, but always checks for conflicting explicit evidence.
This rule is operative for fatwa according to later Hanafis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۲۸ وَاعْمَلُ بِمَفْهُوْمِ رِوَايَاتٍ أَتى * مَا لَمْ يُخَالِفُ لِصَرِيحٍ ثَبَتَا ترجوه : [۲۸] آپ فقہی روایات سے نکلنے والے مفہوم مخالف پر عمل کر سکتے ہیں، جب تک کہ وہ مفہوم مخالف کسی صریح ثابت قول کے خلاف نہ ہو۔
وضاحت : ثَبَتَا" میں الف اشباع کا ہے۔
شرح : یہاں سے علامہ شامی مفہوم مخالف سے ثابت شدہ احکام پر عمل کرنے کی بات ارشاد فرما رہے ہیں، وضاحت اُس کی یہ ہے کہ متاخرین احناف کے نزدیک فقہی روایات معقلی دلائل ، لوگوں کی آپسی گفتگو، حتی کہ حضرات
الله
صحابہ کرام کے وہ اجتہادی اقوال ، جو مدرک بالقیاس ہوں (اس لیے کہ جو اقوال غیر مدرک بالقیاس ہوں گے ، وہ حدیث مرفوع کے حکم میں ہوں گے، اُن میں مفہوم مخالف معتبر نہیں ہوگا ) ؛ ان سب میں مفہوم مخالف معتبر ہے، ان میں مفہوم مخالف سے جو بات مستنبط ہو کر سامنے آئے گی ، اُس پر فتوی دیا جا سکتا ہے، اُس کے مطابق عمل کیا جا سکتا ہے؛ بہ شرطے کہ مفہوم مخالف کے خلاف کوئی صریح حکم نہ پایا جائے ، ایسی صورت میں مفہوم مخالف پر عمل نہیں ہوگا؛ کیوں کہ جو بات صراحتاً ثابت ہو ، وہ اُس کے مقابلے میں رائج ہے، جو کلام سے مفہوم ہو رہی اور مجھی جارہی ہو، مفہوم مخالف کی قدرے وضاحت پیش خدمت ہے۔
28. And act upon the opposite meaning (mafhum mukhalif) of transmitted reports that come, as long as it does not contradict a clear, established [text]. Explanation: In 'thabata' the alif is for prolongation. Commentary: From here, 'Allama Shami instructs acting upon rulings established by mafhum mukhalif. Its explanation is that, according to the later Hanafis, mafhum mukhalif is valid in transmitted reports, rational proofs, people's mutual conversations, and even the ijtihadi statements of the noble Companions that are derivable by qiyas (because statements not derivable by qiyas are like marfu' hadith, in which mafhum mukhalif is not considered). In all these, mafhum mukhalif is valid; whatever is derived from mafhum mukhalif can be issued as a fatwa and acted upon, provided no explicit ruling opposes that mafhum mukhalif. In such a case, mafhum mukhalif will not be acted upon, because what is explicitly established takes precedence over what is implied by speech.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a rasm rule instructing the mufti on the use of mafhum mukhalif (opposite implication). The couplet states that the mufti may act upon mafhum mukhalif derived from fiqh narrations, as long as it does not oppose a clearly established text. The commentary by Ibn 'Abidin (al-Shami) explains that the later Hanafi scholars consider mafhum mukhalif valid in several contexts: in transmitted legal narratives, rational proofs, everyday speech of people, and even in the ijtihadi opinions of the Companions—provided these opinions are based on analogical reasoning (mudrak bil-qiyas). However, if a Companion's statement is not derivable by qiyas, it is treated like a marfu' hadith, and mafhum mukhalif is not considered there. The key condition is that there should be no explicit text (sarih) contradicting the implied meaning; if such exists, the explicit text prevails. This rule helps the mufti extract rulings from broader principles while respecting the primacy of clear, established texts.

Key Terms
مفہوم مخالف (mafhum mukhalif): The implied opposite meaning of a statement; the understanding that the ruling applies to cases other than the mentioned one in an opposite manner.
مدرک بالقیاس (mudrak bil-qiyas): A ruling that can be derived through analogical reasoning; used to distinguish ijtihadi opinions of Companions from those that are considered marfu'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, in which type of Companion statements is mafhum mukhalif NOT considered valid?
A) Those that are mudrak bil-qiyas
B) Those that are not mudrak bil-qiyas
C) All Companion statements
D) Only those that are marfu'

Key Takeaway
The mufti may act upon mafhum mukhalif from various sources, but must always verify that no explicit text contradicts it, and that the source (e.g., Companion statement) is eligible for such implication.

Understanding Mafhum Mukhalif Through Examples and the Hanafi Position
Examples of mafhum mukhalif and beginning of Hanafi rejection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain what mafhum mukhalif (opposite implication) is, identify its use in three illustrative examples from fiqh, the Companions' ijtihad, and rational deduction, and state the Hanafi position that mafhum mukhalif is not an authoritative proof in the speech of the Lawgiver.

Core Idea
Hanafis reject mafhum mukhalif as a definitive proof in the Lawgiver's speech, even though it was used by Companions and in rational arguments.

Mafhum mukhalif (opposite implication) is a linguistic concept where the restriction or condition in a statement implies the opposite ruling for cases not mentioned. This lesson examines three concrete examples from Hanafi texts: the fiqh rule about a woman's braids during ghusl, the Companions' differing ijtihad regarding the Prophet's command to pray Asr only in Bani Qurayza, and Umar's (RA) reasoning about expiation in ihram. We then turn to the Hanafi position that mafhum mukhalif is not valid as a proof in the direct speech of the Lawgiver (Shari'), setting the stage for deeper usul study.

Guided Path
1
Examine the fiqh example of the woman's braids
Identify the explicit ruling: a woman need not undo her braids during ghusl. Derive the mafhum mukhalif: a man with braids must undo them. Note that this example appears in fiqh books as an illustration of opposite implication.
The context is ritual purity, but the principle can extend to other acts.
2
Analyze the Companions' example of praying in Bani Qurayza
The Prophet ordered that no one should pray Asr except in Bani Qurayza. Two groups emerged: one took the literal opposite implication (do not pray on the way, even if time passes); the other interpreted 'except' as indicating haste, so they prayed on time if the time came. Both ijtihads were approved by the Prophet.
This shows that mafhum mukhalif was considered by some Companions, but not as a binding proof.
3
Explore Umar's example with the predatory animal
Umar (RA) killed a predatory animal in ihram and offered expiation, explaining 'We initiated the attack.' The mafhum mukhalif is: if one does not initiate (i.e., the animal attacks first), no expiation is required. This rational proof relies on opposite implication to derive the ruling.
Umar's own action and explanation are used as a precedent, but note the difference between a Companion's speech and the Lawgiver's speech.
4
Consider the Hanafi position on mafhum mukhalif in the Lawgiver's speech
The Hanafi school states that mafhum mukhalif is not an authoritative proof in the speech of the Lawgiver (Shari'). The reason, as partially given in the passage, is that explicit mention of something does not necessarily imply its opposite. The full reasoning is not provided here but will be covered in advanced usul texts.
This is a foundational rule in Hanafi usul al-fiqh, distinguishing them from schools that give mafhum mukhalif more weight.

صحابہ کرام کے اقوال میں مفہوم مخالف کے معتبر ہونے کی مثال
حدیث شریف میں ہے کہ ایک موقع پر آپ عالم نے حضرات صحابہ ے فرمایا: لا يُصَلَّبَنَّ أَحَدٌ الْعَصْرَ إِلَّا فِي بَنِي قُرَيْظَةَ " (سب کے سے سب کو عصر کی نماز بنوقیظہ میں پڑھنی ہے ) ، حضرات صحابہ صلى الله عليه وسلم کے اجتہادات اس موقع سے بدل گئے ، بعضوں نے لفظ کے ظاہری معنی کو سامنے رکھتے ہوئے یہ نتیجہ نکالا کہ بنو قریظہ میں ہی نماز پڑھنی ہے؛ اس کا مفہوم مخالف یہ ہوا کہ اس سے پہلے راستے میں نماز نہیں پڑھنی ہے؛ اگر چہ نماز کا وقت ختم ہو جائے۔
دوسرے بعض نے نماز کو وقت پر ادا کرنے سے متعلق نصوص کے پیش نظر آپ سا لن لینے کے مبارک ارشاد کا مطلب یہ سمجھا کہ تجھیل مراد ہے، یہ نہیں کے نماز کا وقت نکلتا بھی رہے، تو بھی بنو قریظہ میں ہی نماز پڑھنی ہے، اس کا مفہوم مخالف یہ نکلا کہ اگر راستے میں نماز کا وقت ہو جائے ، تو نماز پڑھ لینی ہے، آپ مالی ایم کے حکم کی خلاف ورزی نہیں ہوگی ؛ دونوں جماعت نے اپنے اپنے اجتہاد پر عمل کیا اور آپ ام نے دونوں کے اجتہاد کی تصویب فرمائی۔
چند دلائل عقلیہ میں مفہوم مخالف کے معتبر ہونے کی مثال فقہ کی کتابوں میں یہ جزئیہ ہے کہ اگر کوئی درندہ محرم پر حملہ کر دے اور محرم حالت احرام میں اپنے بچاؤ کے لیے اُسے قتل کر ڈالے، تو محرم پر کوئی دم وغیرہ واجب نہیں، پھر اس کی دلیل یہ دی جاتی ہے: ایک مرتبہ حضرت عمر یہ حالت احرام میں ایک درندے کو مارڈالا، پھر بہ طور دم ایک مینڈھے کی قربانی دی اور اپنے اس عمل کی علت یہ بیان فرمائی کہ " انا ابتد انسان یعنی ہم نے درندے پر حملے کا اقدام کیا تھا۔ اس علت سے یہ حکم مفہوم ہوا کہ اگر محرم اقدام نہ کرے؛ بل کہ درندہ جب حملہ کرے، تو محرم اپنے دفاع میں اُسے مار ڈالے، تو پھر ایسی صورت میں کوئی دم واجب نہ ہوگا ؛ اگر حضرت عمر دانہ کے ارشاد کا یہ مفہوم نہ لیا جائے، تو پھر حضرت کے قول " انا ابتدأناہ کی تعلیل کا کوئی فائدہ نہیں رہے گا۔

The speaker will be liable for one hundred rupees. It is apparent that this ruling of acknowledgment is based on the opposite implication. Example of the validity of opposite implication in jurisprudential narrations: In the books of fiqh, there is this case: 'It is not obligatory for a woman to undo her braids' (and at the time of ritual bath, it is not necessary for a woman to open her braided hair). From this it is understood that here the restriction to woman intends to exclude men (if a man has long hair and has braided it, then during the major impurity bath, it is obligatory for him to open the braids and wash). Example of the validity of opposite implication in the statements of the Companions: In a noble hadith, on one occasion the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) told the Companions: 'None of you should pray Asr except in Bani Qurayza' (everyone must pray Asr in Bani Qurayza). The ijtihads of the Companions differed on this occasion. Some, considering the apparent meaning of the words, concluded that prayer must be performed in Bani Qurayza; its opposite implication is that prayer should not be performed earlier on the way, even if the time for prayer passes. Others, considering the texts about performing prayer on time, understood the Prophet's blessed command to mean haste, not that even if the time for prayer passes, it should be performed in Bani Qurayza. Its opposite implication is that if the time for prayer arrives on the way, then prayer should be performed; there will be no violation of the Prophet's command. Both groups acted upon their own ijtihad, and the Prophet approved both ijtihads. Example of the validity of opposite implication in rational proofs: In the books of fiqh, there is this case: If a predatory animal attacks a person in ihram, and the person kills it in self-defense while in the state of ihram, then no expiation (blood) is obligatory. Then its proof is given: Once Umar (رضی الله عنه) killed a predatory animal while in ihram, then he sacrificed a ram as expiation and explained the cause of his action: 'We initiated the attack' (i.e., we took the initiative to attack). From this cause, the opposite implication is that if the person in ihram does not take initiative but the animal attacks and he kills it in self-defense, then no expiation is obligatory. If this opposite implication of Umar's statement is not taken, then the explanation of the cause in his saying 'We initiated it' would have no benefit. Why is opposite implication not considered authoritative in the legal texts of the Hanafis? According to the Hanafis, opposite implication is not considered authoritative in the speech of the Lawgiver because from the explicit mention of something in the speech of the Lawgiver, it does not necessarily follow that its

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the concept of mafhūm mukhālif (opposite implication) with examples. It first gives a fiqh example: the ruling that a woman need not undo her braids during ghusl implies, by opposite implication, that a man must undo his braids if he has them. Then it gives an example from the Companions' ijtihad regarding the Prophet's order to pray Asr only in Bani Qurayza; some Companions understood it literally and did not pray on the way, while others interpreted it as haste and prayed on time if the time came. Both were approved. Then a rational example: Umar's statement that he initiated an attack on a predatory animal in ihram implies that if one does not initiate, no expiation is required. Finally, the passage poses the question: why do Hanafis not consider mafhūm mukhālif authoritative in the speech of the Lawgiver? The answer is cut off, but it begins to explain that explicit mention does not necessarily entail its opposite.

Key Terms
مفہوم مخالف (mafhūm mukhālif): The inferred opposite meaning from a text; e.g., if the text specifies a condition or restriction for one case, the opposite is inferred for other cases not mentioned.
احناف (hanafī): Followers of the Hanafi school of law, one of the four Sunni schools, founded by Imam Abu Hanifa.
نصوص شرعیہ (nusūs sharʿiyyah): Legal texts of the Shari'ah, primarily the Quran and Sunnah.

Checkpoint
In the fiqh example of the woman's braids during ghusl, what is the mafhum mukhalif?
A) A woman must undo her braids during ghusl.
B) A man must undo his braids during ghusl.
C) Neither men nor women need to undo braids.
D) Only women need to undo braids.

Key Takeaway
Mafhum mukhalif is not an authoritative proof in the speech of the Lawgiver according to the Hanafi school, even though it may be used in other contexts such as rational arguments or the statements of non-Lawgivers.

Mefhum al-Mukhalif: Its Validity in Hanafi Usul al-Ifta
Explains the Hanafi position on mefhum al-mukhalif with proofs and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contexts in which Hanafis accept mefhum al-mukhalif (opposite implication) and apply the condition for its validity in issuing fatwas.

Core Idea
Mefhum al-mukhalif is valid in rational proofs and ordinary speech, but not in the direct words of Allah and His Prophet.

This passage clarifies a fundamental point in Hanafi usul al-ifta: the opposite implication (mefhum al-mukhalif) is not automatically rejected by the school. Instead, its validity depends on the type of speech being considered. Understanding this distinction is essential for a mufti to correctly derive rulings from sources of varying authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of speech discussed
The passage distinguishes between the speech of the Lawgiver (Shari'), the statements of ordinary people, Sahaba statements that are subject to ijtihad, and rational proofs. Note each category.
2
Examine the proofs for each category
For the Lawgiver, the Quranic verse 4:23 is cited to show that restrictions may be for eloquence, not negation. For rational proofs, the example of Umar's statement about killing a predator in ihram is given.
3
Understand the condition for validity
Later Hanafis condition the use of mefhum al-mukhalif on the absence of any explicit contrary instance or statement.
4
Apply to fatwa practice
A mufti must assess whether the source of a questioned ruling is from the Lawgiver (no opposite implication) or from human reasoning (possible).

جہاں مفہوم مخالف معتبر ہے، وہاں شرط کے ساتھ معتبر ہے متاخرین علمائے احناف نے مفہوم مخالف کو جہاں بھی معتبر مانا ہے، وہ اس قید کے ساتھ مقید ہے کہ اس کے خلاف کوئی صریح جزئیہ یا قول موجود نہ ہو، "مالم
Even if they remain, they should still pray in Banu Qurayza; its opposite implication is that if prayer time occurs on the way, they should pray, and there will be no violation of the Prophet's command. Both groups acted on their own ijtihad, and the Prophet approved both. [Then] An example of the validity of opposite implication in rational proofs in fiqh books: if a predatory animal attacks a person in ihram and the person kills it in self-defense, no compensation (dam) is obligatory. The proof is that once Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) killed a predatory animal in ihram, then sacrificed a ram as compensation, and gave the reason 'We initiated the attack.' From this reason, it is understood that if the person did not initiate but the animal attacked, then killing it in self-defense does not incur compensation. If this implication is not taken, then the reasoning in Umar's statement 'We initiated it' would be useless. Why is opposite implication not authoritative in the Shari' texts according to Hanafis? Because in the speech of the Lawgiver, mentioning a specific thing does not necessarily imply that the ruling is negated for other than it, since the speech of the Lawgiver is a treasury of eloquence; mentioning something specifically may be for another purpose. For example, Allah's saying: '...and your stepdaughters who are in your guardianship' (4:23). The benefit of the restriction 'in your guardianship' is that generally the children from a previous husband come under the guardianship of the new husband. Taking the opposite implication, no one holds that if the girl is not under the new husband's guardianship, then marriage to her is permissible. As for the speech of ordinary people, it is devoid of such qualities, so opposite implication can be used in their speech. An important note: The opposite implication considered valid in the sayings of the Companions refers to those sayings that are subject to analogical reasoning (ma'drak bil-qiyas), based on reflection, not those that are not subject to analogical reasoning (ghair ma'drak bil-qiyas), which cannot be understood by reason, because such speech is like a marfu' hadith and counts as the speech of the Lawgiver, and it has been established that opposite implication is not valid in the Lawgiver's speech. Where opposite implication is valid, it is with a condition: the later Hanafi scholars have conditioned its validity on the absence of any explicit contrary instance or statement, "Malam..."
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the Hanafi position on the validity of mefhum al-mukhalif (opposite implication) in the context of usul al-ifta. The author begins by referencing the well-known incident of the Banu Qurayza, where the Prophet's command to pray 'asr at Banu Qurayza was interpreted differently by two groups: one praying on the way and the other delaying until arrival. The Prophet approved both, indicating that opposite implication from his words (i.e., if prayer time occurs on the way, it is permissible to pray) is not necessarily authoritative because his speech can have multiple valid interpretations. The passage then gives an example from fiqh: if a predator attacks a muhrim (one in ihram) and the muhrim kills it in self-defense, no dam (penalty) is required. The proof relies on the statement of Umar (may Allah be pleased with him), who explained that he initiated the attack, so he had to pay compensation. The opposite implication is that if the muhrim did not initiate but merely defended, no compensation is required. This reasoning is considered valid in rational proofs (dalil 'aqli) because Umar's statement is not the direct speech of the Lawgiver. Next, the author explains why Hanafis do not accept mefhum al-mukhalif in the Shari' texts: because the Lawgiver's speech is a treasury of eloquence (balaghah), and mentioning a specific condition may have other rhetorical purposes (e.g., describing the usual case) rather than negating the ruling for others. The example of the Quranic verse about stepdaughters 'in your guardianship' is given: the restriction is not meant to permit marriage to stepdaughters not under guardianship; rather, it describes the common scenario. Ordinary people's speech lacks such eloquence, so opposite implication is valid in their statements. An important note clarifies that in the sayings of the Companions, mefhum al-mukhalif is only valid for those statements that are subject to analogical reasoning (i.e., based on ijtihad), not for those that are not (such as matters known only by revelation), because the latter are treated like the direct speech of the Lawgiver, where opposite implication is not accepted. Finally, the passage notes that according to later Hanafi scholars, wherever they do accept mefhum al-mukhalif, it is conditional upon the absence of any explicit contrary evidence (sarih juz'i or qawl). This passage is a methodological rule (rasm al-mufti) about the use of mefhum al-mukhalif in issuing fatwas, distinguishing between divine and human speech.

Key Terms
مفہوم مخالف (mefhum al-mukhalif): Opposite implication: deriving a ruling opposite to the explicitly stated one from a restricted phrase.
مدرک بالقیاس (mudrak bil-qiyas): A statement that is understood through analogical reasoning and reflection, not through direct revelation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, in which type of statement is mefhum al-mukhalif considered valid by Hanafis?
A) The direct speech of Allah (Quran)
B) The direct speech of the Prophet (hadith)
C) The statements of ordinary people
D) Any statement, regardless of source, as long as it is clear

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position on mefhum al-mukhalif is nuanced: it is accepted in rational proofs, ordinary speech, and ijtihadi statements of Sahaba, but not in the direct speech of the Lawgiver, and always conditioned on the absence of contrary evidence.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

Mafhum Mukhalif: Conditions for Validity in Hanafi Fatwa
Rasm al-mufti rule on mafhum mukhalif validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi condition that mafhum mukhalif is valid only when no explicit contradictory text (sarih) exists, and identify when a Companion's statement is mudrak bil-qiyas.

Core Idea
Mafhum mukhalif yields to explicit text — sarih overrides inference in Hanafi fatwa.

Mafhum mukhalif (opposite inference) is a powerful tool in usul, but its application in Hanafi fatwa is carefully restricted. This lesson unpacks the conditions that later Hanafi scholars placed on its use, particularly the requirement that no explicit contradictory ruling exists. You will learn why Shari' speech often does not yield opposite meanings, and how to handle Companions' statements and apparent contradictions in the madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish Shari' speech from human speech
Identify that mafhum mukhalif is generally not valid in the speech of the Lawgiver because it is the mine of eloquence; conditions mentioned may be for common occurrence, not restriction. In human speech, it is valid.
Quran 4:23 is the key example: 'stepdaughters in your care' does not imply permissibility outside care.
2
Apply the condition to Companion statements
Determine whether a Companion's statement is mudrak bil-qiyas (based on reasoning) or ghayr mudrak bil-qiyas (direct transmission). Only the former can yield mafhum mukhalif; the latter is treated like marfu' hadith.
3
Recognize the overriding condition from the muta'akhkhirin
Later Hanafi scholars condition mafhum mukhalif on the absence of any explicit contradictory text (sarih). If a clear ruling exists, mafhum is abandoned.
The wudu example from al-Hidayah vs. Tuhfat al-Fuqaha' illustrates this.
4
Analyze the furu' example
Review the hand-washing before entering the vessel: al-Hidayah's mention of 'from sleep' would yield a mafhum that it's only sunnah for such a person, but explicit texts in Tuhfat and al-Muhit state it as general sunnah, so the mafhum is overridden.
This shows the hierarchy: explicit text > mafhum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فائدہ اُس کے علاوہ سے حکم کی نفی ہی ہو؛ اس لیے کہ کلام شارع بلاغت کا مخزن ہے، کسی چیز کا خصوصیت سے کلام شارع میں تذکرہ بھی کسی اور مقصد سے بھی ہوتا ہے۔ جیسے ارشاد باری ہے: ﴿وَرَائِبُكُمُ الَّتِي فِي حُجُورِكُمْ﴾ [النساء: ٢٣] (تمھارے لیے حرام ہیں تمھاری بیویوں کی وہ بیٹیاں، جو تمھاری پرورش میں ہیں۔) اس آیت میں ﴿حُجُورِكُم﴾ کی قید کا فائدہ بالا جماع یہی ہے کہ عام طور پر یہی ہوتا ہے کہ بیوی کی سابق شوہر کی اولاد، دوسرے شوہر کی پرورش میں آجاتی ہے۔ آیت شریفہ کا مفہوم مخالف مراد لیتے ہوئے کسی کے نزدیک بھی یہ مطلب نہیں کہ اگر وہ لڑ کی موجودہ شوہر کی پرورش میں نہ ہو، تو اُس سے نکاح جائز ہے۔ اور جہاں تک لوگوں کے کلام کی بات ہے، تو وہ اس طرح کی خوبیوں سے خالی ہوتا ہے؛ لہذا اُن کے کلام میں مفہوم مخالف سے استدلال کیا جائے گا۔

ایک اہم نوٹ: حضرات صحابہ رضوان اللہ علیہم اجمعین کے اقوال میں جو مفہوم مخالف معتبر ہے، اس سے مراد وہ اقوال ہیں، جو مدرک بالقیاس ہوں، جن کا مدار غور و فکر پر ہو۔ وہ اقوال مراد نہیں، جو غیر مدرک بالقیاس ہوں، جو عقل سے نہ سمجھے جا سکتے ہوں؛ اس لیے کہ ایسا کلام تو حدیث مرفوع کے حکم میں ہوگا اور کلام شارع میں شمار کیا جائے گا اور یہ بات پہلے آچکی کہ کلام شارع میں مفہوم مخالف معتبر نہیں۔

جہاں مفہوم مخالف معتبر ہے، وہاں شرط کے ساتھ معتبر ہے۔ متاخرین علمائے احناف نے مفہوم مخالف کو جہاں بھی معتبر مانا ہے، وہ اس قید کے ساتھ مقید ہے کہ اس کے خلاف کوئی صریح جزئیہ یا قول موجود نہ ہو، "مالم يخالف لصريح ثبت"۔ علامہ شامی نے اسی بات کو بیان فرمایا ہے: ہدایہ وغیرہ میں ہے: "وسنن الطهارة: غسل اليدين قبل إدخالهما الإناء إذا استيقظ المتوضيء من نومه ...." (نیند سے بیدار ہونے والے شخص کے لیے یہ مسنون ہے کہ وہ جب وضو کا ارادہ کرے، تو پانی کے برتن میں ہاتھ ڈالنے سے پہلے دونوں ہاتھوں کو گٹوں تک دھولے)، جس کا مفہوم مخالف یہ نکلا کہ وضو کا ارادہ کرنے والا شخص اگر نیند سے بیدار نہیں ہوا ہے، تو پھر اُس کے لیے پانی کے برتن میں ہاتھ ڈالنے سے پہلے دونوں ہاتھوں کو گٹوں تک دھونا مسنون نہیں۔ مگر اس کے بالمقابل "تحفة الفقهاء" اور "المحيط البرهاني" میں یہ مسئلہ صاف طور پر اس طرح لکھا ہے کہ وضو شروع کرتے وقت، دونوں ہاتھوں کو برتن میں ڈالنے سے پہلے گٹوں تک دھونا مسنون ہے؛ ان دونوں کتابوں میں نیند سے بیدار ہونے، نہ ہونے کی کوئی قید نہیں۔

دونوں کتابوں کی عبارت ملاحظہ فرمائیں:
"والثالث: غسل اليدين إلى الرسغين لإدخالهما في الإناء احترازا عن توهم النجاسة." (تحفة الفقهاء: ١٢/١)
"إن كان الإناء صغيراً، يمكنه رفعه، لا يُدخِل يده فيه؛ بل يرفعه بشماله ويصبه على كفه اليمنى ويغسلها ثلثاً، ثم يأخذ الإناء بيمينه ويصب الماء على كفه اليسرى ويغسلها ثلثاً، وإن كان الإناء كبيراً لا يمكن رفعه ..... فإن كان معه كوز صغير يرفع الماء بالكوز ولا يدخل يده فيه......" (المحيط البرهاني، الطهارات الفصل الأول في الوضوء: ١٧٩/١)
Benefit: (Specifying something) only negates a ruling for what is other than it? No; because the speech of the Lawgiver is the mine of eloquence; mentioning a thing specifically in the speech of the Lawgiver might also be for another purpose. For example, the saying of Allah Most High: 'and your stepdaughters who are in your care' [Surah al-Nisa 23] (Forbidden to you are the daughters of your wives who are under your care.) In this verse, the restriction 'in your care' indicates what usually happens, i.e., that the stepdaughter usually comes under the care of the new husband. Taking the opposite meaning of the noble verse, no one would understand that if a girl is not under the care of the current husband, then marrying her is permissible. As for the speech of people, it is devoid of such qualities; therefore, in their speech, one may argue using opposite meaning. An important note: Among the statements of the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them), the opposite meaning that is considered valid refers to those statements that are based on reasoning (mudrak bil-qiyas), whose basis is reflection and reasoning. It does not refer to those statements that are not based on reasoning, which cannot be understood by the intellect; because such speech will be in the category of a marfu' hadith and will be counted as the speech of the Lawgiver, and it has already been mentioned that opposite meaning is not considered valid in the speech of the Lawgiver. Wherever opposite meaning is considered valid, it is valid with a condition. The later Hanafi scholars have restricted their acceptance of opposite meaning to the condition that no clear particular instance or statement exists contrary to it, 'as long as it does not contradict an explicit established text'. 'Allamah Shami has stated this. In al-Hidayah and others: 'And the sunan of purification: washing the hands before inserting them into the vessel when the person performing wudu wakes from sleep...' The opposite meaning derived from this is that if a person intending wudu has not woken from sleep, then washing the hands up to the wrists before inserting them into the water vessel is not sunnah. However, in contrast, in Tuhfat al-Fuqaha' and al-Muhit al-Burhani, the ruling is clearly written as follows: at the beginning of wudu, it is sunnah to wash the hands up to the wrists before inserting them into the vessel; these two books do not mention any restriction regarding waking from sleep or not. Observe the texts of both books: 'And the third (sunnah): washing the hands up to the wrists before inserting them into the vessel, to avoid the assumption of impurity.' (Tuhfat al-Fuqaha', 1:12) 'If the vessel is small, and he can lift it, he should not put his hand into it; rather he should lift it with his left hand and pour water onto his right palm and wash it three times, then take the vessel with his right hand and pour water onto his left palm and wash it three times. If the vessel is large and cannot be lifted...' (al-Muhit al-Burhani, Taharat, first chapter on wudu, 1:179)
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a rasm al-mufti rule about the validity of mafhum mukhalif (opposite inference) in fatwa. The author distinguishes between the speech of the Lawgiver (Shari') and human speech. In Shari' speech, a qualification does not necessarily imply the opposite because the Lawgiver's speech is highly eloquent and may include a condition due to common occurrence, not to restrict the ruling. The example given is the Quranic verse (4:23) mentioning stepdaughters 'in your care'; this does not mean stepdaughters not in one's care are permissible to marry. In human speech, however, qualifications do imply the opposite because human speech lacks such eloquence. A key nuance: for statements of the Companions, mafhum mukhalif is valid only if the statement is based on reasoning (mudrak bil-qiyas), not if it is a direct transmission (ghayr mudrak bil-qiyas), which is treated like the Prophet's speech. The passage then states that later Hanafi scholars (muta'akhkhirin) limit acceptance of mafhum mukhalif to cases where no explicit contradictory text exists. The wudu example illustrates this: al-Hidayah mentions washing hands before entering the vessel is sunnah specifically for one who wakes from sleep, yielding the opposite that it is not sunnah for others. But Tuhfat al-Fuqaha' and al-Muhit al-Burhani declare it sunnah generally without the sleep condition, so the explicit text overrides the mafhum. This is a critical refinement: mafhum mukhalif is a tool but yields to explicit evidence.

Key Terms
مَفْهُوم مُخَالِف (mafhum mukhalif): An inference from a text that the ruling applies only when the mentioned condition exists; absent the condition, the opposite ruling applies.
مُدْرَك بِالْقِيَاس (mudrak bil-qiyas): A legal ruling or statement that is derived through analogical reasoning (qiyas), based on human reflection and inference from texts.
صَرِيح (sarih): An explicit, unambiguous text or statement that directly gives a ruling, overriding any inferential meaning like mafhum mukhalif.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later Hanafi scholars (muta'akhkhirin), what condition must be met for mafhum mukhalif to be considered valid?
A) It must be from the speech of the Lawgiver directly.
B) There must be no explicit contradictory text or ruling.
C) It must be from the statements of the Companions only.
D) It must be based on a weak hadith.

Key Takeaway
Mafhum mukhalif is conditionally valid in Hanafi fatwa: not in Shari' speech, only in mudrak bil-qiyas from Companions, and always subject to being overridden by any explicit contradictory text.

Priority of Explicit Text over Mafhum Mukhalif
Illustrating that mafhum mukhalif is overridden by an explicit text

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a mafhum mukhalif is overridden by an explicit text (sarih juz'i) and apply this rule in fatwa.

Core Idea
An explicit text overrides any opposite implication, no matter how plausible.

A mufti often encounters texts that imply a ruling by their conditions. But these implications hold only when no explicit text directly addresses the case. This lesson illustrates that rule with a fiqh example from wudu, showing how later authorities resolved an apparent conflict between a conditional statement and an unconditional explicit text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and its mafhum mukhalif
Read the Hidayah's statement: 'washing hands before insertion is sunnah for one who wakes from sleep.' The mafhum mukhalif is that it is not sunnah for one who did not wake from sleep.
The mafhum is the opposite implication of the condition.
2
Locate the explicit text on the same case
Find the unconditional ruling in Tuhfat al-Fuqaha and al-Muhit al-Burhani: washing hands before insertion is sunnah without any condition.
This explicit text directly addresses the same specific action.
3
Compare the two texts
Note that the explicit text lacks the condition present in the first text. The explicit text is a sarih juz'i (explicit particular ruling).
4
Apply the rule
Since there is an explicit text on the same issue, the mafhum mukhalif is disregarded. The ruling follows the explicit text: washing hands is sunnah for everyone.
The principle: a mafhum cannot override a sarih juz'i.
5
Consider the tanbih (note about restrictive vs incidental condition)
Some scholars view the condition in Hidayah as restrictive (ihtirazi) rather than incidental (ittifaqi). In that case, the mafhum would be intended, but the example would not fit. However, the rule remains that even if the condition is restrictive, an explicit text on the same issue overrides it.
The tanbih clarifies that the example's validity depends on interpretation of the condition, but the rule itself is independent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يخالف لصریح ثبتا سے علامہ شامی نے اسی بات کو بیان فرمایا ہے: ہدایہ وغیرہ میں ہے: "وسُننُ الطهارةِ غَسل اليدين قبل إدخالهما الإناء إذا استيقظ المتوضيء من نومه .... اهـ" (نیند سے بیدار ہونے والے شخص کے لیے یہ مسنون ہے کہ وہ جب وضو کا ارادہ کرے، تو پانی کے برتن میں ہاتھ ڈالنے سے پہلے دونوں ہاتھوں کو گٹوں تک دھولے ) ، جس کا مفہوم مخالف یہ نکلا کہ وضو کا ارادہ کرنے والا شخص اگر نیند سے بیدار نہیں ہوا ہے ، تو پھر اُس کے لیے پانی کے برتن میں ہاتھ ڈالنے سے پہلے دونوں ہاتھوں کو گٹوں تک دھونا مسنون نہیں ، مگر اس کے بالمقابل "تحفة الفقهاء "اور " المحيط البرهاني" میں یہ مسئلہ صاف طور پر اس طرح لکھا ہے کہ وضو شروع کرتے وقت ، دونوں ہاتھوں کو برتن میں ڈالنے سے پہلے گٹوں تک دھونا مسنون ہے؛ ان دونوں کتابوں میں نیند سے بیدار ہونے ، نہ ہونے کی کوئی قید نہیں۔ دونوں کتابوں کی عبارت ملاحظہ فرمائیں: . والثالث : غسل اليدين إلى الرسغين لإدخالهما في الإناء احترازا عن توهم النجاسة . (تحفة الفقهاء: ۱۲/۱) • إن كان الإناء صغيراً ، يمكنه رفعه ، لا يُدخِل يده فيه ؛ بل يرفعه بشماله ويصبه على كفه اليمنى ويغسلها ثلثاً، ثم يأخذ الإناء بيمينه ويصب الماء على كفه اليسرى ويغسلها ثلثاً ، وإن كان الإناء كبيراً لا يمكن رفعه ..... فإن كان معه كوز صغير يرفع الماء بالكوز ولا يدخل يده فيه......اهـ. (المحيط البرهاني، الطهارات الفصل الأول في الوضوء: ١٧٩/١) لہذا ہدایہ وغیرہ کی عبارت کا جو مفہوم مخالف نکل رہا تھا: "نیند سے بیدار نہ ہو نے والے کے لیے وضو سے پہلے دونوں ہاتھوں کے گٹوں تک دھونے کا مسنون نہ ہونا ؛ اب اس صریح جزیے کے مقابلے میں اُس کا اعتبار نہیں ہوگا اور وضو کا ارادہ کرنے والے کے لیے ، خواہ وہ نیند سے بیدار ہونے والا ہو یا نہ ہو؛ دونوں کے لیے برابر درجے میں پانی کے برتن میں ہاتھ ڈالنے سے پہلے دونوں ہاتھوں کا گٹوں تک دھونا مسنون رہے گا۔ تنبیہ: یہ مثال اس وقت ہے، جب ہدایہ وغیرہ کی عبارت میں "إذا استيقظ من نومه" کی قید کو اتفاقی مانا جائے ؛ ورنہ بعض علماء نے اس قید کو احترازی مانا ہے، اس صورت میں یہ جزئیہ مثال نہیں بن سکتا، باقی حکم تو وہی رہے گا کہ صریح جزیے کے مقابلے میں مفہوم مخالف کا اعتبار نہیں۔
Allamah Shami explained the same point from [a statement] that contradicts an explicit confirmed text: In Hidayah etc.: "And among the sunan of purification is washing the hands before inserting them into the vessel when the one performing wudu wakes from sleep..." (end). Its opposite implication (mafhum mukhalif) is that if a person intending wudu did not wake from sleep, then washing the hands up to the wrists before inserting them into the vessel is not sunnah. But in contrast to this, in Tuhfat al-Fuqaha and al-Muhit al-Burhani, this issue is clearly written as follows: At the start of wudu, before inserting the hands into the vessel, washing the hands up to the wrists is sunnah. In these two books, there is no condition of waking from sleep or not. Observe the text of both books: "And the third: washing the hands up to the wrists for inserting them into the vessel, as a precaution against the imagination of impurity." (Tuhfat al-Fuqaha 1/12) "If the vessel is small and he can lift it, he should not insert his hand into it; rather, he lifts it with his left hand and pours water onto his right palm and washes it three times, then takes the vessel with his right hand and pours water onto his left palm and washes it three times. If the vessel is large and cannot be lifted... if he has a small jug, he lifts water with the jug and does not insert his hand into it..." (al-Muhit al-Burhani, Taharat, first chapter on wudu, 1/179) Therefore, the mafhum mukhalif derived from the text of Hidayah – that for one who did not wake from sleep, washing the hands up to the wrists before wudu is not sunnah – will not be considered in the face of this explicit text. And for the one intending wudu, whether he woke from sleep or not, it remains sunnah to wash both hands up to the wrists before inserting them into the vessel. Note: This example applies when the condition "if he wakes from sleep" in Hidayah is considered incidental; otherwise, some scholars consider this condition as restrictive. In that case, this specific point cannot serve as an example, but the ruling remains that mafhum mukhalif is not considered against an explicit text.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is an illustration of a methodological rule for muftis: when a mafhum mukhalif (opposite implication) derived from a condition conflicts with an explicit text (sarih juz'i) directly addressing the same case, the explicit text takes precedence and the mafhum is disregarded. The example is from the fiqh of wudu: The Hidayah states that washing hands before inserting them into the water vessel is a sunnah when one wakes from sleep. The mafhum mukhalif suggests it is not sunnah for someone who did not wake from sleep. However, explicit texts from Tuhfat al-Fuqaha and al-Muhit al-Burhani state without any condition that washing the hands before insertion is sunnah for everyone. Since these are explicit rulings on the very same situation, the mafhum is overridden. The commentary adds a tanbih: This example is valid only if the condition in Hidayah is considered incidental (ittifaqi) rather than restrictive (ihtirazi). If it is restrictive, then the mafhum is part of the intended meaning, but even then the principle stands that a mafhum cannot override an explicit text. The author emphasizes that the rule remains: a mafhum mukhalif is not considered against a sarih juz'i.

Key Terms
مفہوم مخالف (mafhum mukhalif): The opposite implication derived from a condition or attribute in a text (e.g., 'if X, then Y' implies 'if not X, then not Y').
صریح جزئیہ (sarih juz'i): An explicit textual ruling that directly addresses a specific situation, without relying on implication.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Consideration of Custom (Urf) in Fatwa
Poetic rule with commentary establishing custom as a valid source for fatwa with a condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-urf fi al-shar'i lahu i'tibar' to identify when a custom can serve as a basis for a fatwa and when it cannot.

Core Idea
Custom has weight in Shariah, but never overrides a clear divine text.

In issuing fatwas, muftis must navigate between fixed Shariah texts and changing human customs. This lesson introduces a foundational maxim: custom is legally considered, provided it does not contradict the Shariah. The following verse and commentary from Allamah Shami guide the mufti in this balance.

Guided Path
1
State the Maxim
Identify the poetic verse: 'Al-urf fi al-shar'i lahu i'tibar' — custom has consideration in the Shariah.
2
Note the Condition
Observe the condition: custom is valid only as long as it does not oppose the Shariah (ma lam yukhalif al-shar').
3
Recognize the Scope
Understand that this maxim primarily governs mu'amalat (transactions) and 'adat (customs), but also applies to ibadat in secondary aspects.
4
Apply to a Furu' Case
Apply the maxim to a concrete example, such as customary delivery in sales, and see how custom determines the ruling.

Discussion of custom: (19) And custom has consideration in the Shariah; therefore, rulings are often based upon it. Translation: In the Shariah, custom is also considered (in the context of giving fatwa); this is why rulings are also based on it. Explanation: Through this verse, Allamah Shami provides guidance for fatwa-writing that when giving fatwa, the muftis must take into account the custom and habit of the people, as long as they do not go against the Shariah. This is because custom is given consideration in the Shariah and rulings are established upon it.

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental rule for muftis: custom (urf) is a valid source for establishing legal rulings, provided it does not contradict explicit scriptural texts. The poetic verse states that custom has consideration (i'tibar) in the Shariah, and rulings are frequently based upon it. The explanation emphasizes that muftis must be aware of the customs of their time and place when issuing fatwas, because Islamic law takes custom into account. For example, in transactions, what constitutes 'customary' delivery or payment terms can affect the ruling. The condition is explicit: custom is only valid so long as it does not oppose the Shariah. This aligns with the Hanafi principle that accepted custom (urf sahih) can serve as a basis for rulings in areas not directly covered by nass, and can even specify general texts.

Key Terms
عرف (urf): Custom or habitual practice of people that does not contradict the Shariah.

The Role of Custom ('Urf) in Issuing Fatwas
Couplet and commentary establishing the authority of custom in fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'urf and 'adah, distinguish between them, and apply the principle that custom is a valid source for fatwa when not contradicting Shariah.

Core Idea
Custom ('urf) is a valid basis for fatwa as long as it does not contradict explicit Shariah texts.

This passage from 'Aun al-Mughni' explains a crucial principle in Hanafi fatwa methodology: the consideration of custom. The couplet by Allamah Shami establishes that rulings may be based on 'urf. The commentary then defines and distinguishes 'urf from mere habit ('adah). Understanding this distinction is essential for a mufti to apply custom correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle stated in the couplet
Read the couplet: 'والعرف في الشرع له اعتبار'. What does it say about the status of 'urf? It states that custom has consideration (i'tibar) in Shariah.
This is the core maxim.
2
Define 'urf and 'adah from the text
From the commentary, write the definitions: 'urf is what is settled in minds by reason and accepted by sound natures; 'adah is a repeating matter without rational connection.
Be precise about the wording.
3
Distinguish between 'urf and 'adah
Note that 'adah is broader: it includes any repeated action, even irrational. 'Urf is a subset: it must be rationally acceptable and prevalent among sound minds.
This distinction is subtle but important for fatwa.
4
Apply the condition for using 'urf in fatwa
The commentary states custom is considered 'as long as it is not against the Shariah'. Apply this condition: when issuing a fatwa, the mufti must verify that the custom does not contradict a clear text.
Custom cannot override a definitive proof.
5
Recognize the terminology usage among scholars
Note that fuqaha often use 'adah synonymously with 'urf, but usul scholars distinguish. This affects how one reads fiqh texts.
Be aware of context.

Discussion of custom (urf). 29. And custom in the Shariah has consideration; therefore rulings may be based upon it. Translation: [19] And in the Shariah, custom is also considered (in issuing fatwas); this is why rulings are sometimes based on it. Commentary: Through this couplet, Allamah Shami gives guidance for fatwa writing: when issuing a fatwa, it is necessary for muftis to observe the custom and habit of the people, as long as it is not against the Shariah, because custom is given consideration in the Shariah and rulings are built upon it. Definition of custom and habit: Allamah in the commentary gave two definitions: 'Urf and 'adah: that which is settled in the minds from the aspect of reason and which sound natures accept. (The terms 'urf and 'adah apply to that saying or action which becomes established in people's hearts by reason and which serious-natured individuals accept.) Al-'adah: a repeating matter without a rational connection. (Habit is called that saying or action which is done repeatedly without a rational cause, without difficulty.) Difference between 'urf and 'adah: By mentioning these two definitions, Allamah Shami's objective is to explain a subtle difference: 'adah has generality; every action repeated over and over without cause is called 'adah, whether reason accepts it or not. For example, a person picks up a word on his tongue and repeats it without reason during speech. 'Urf has some specificity: 'urf is that 'adah which becomes prevalent among people by the requirement of reason and which serious natures accept. Fuqaha generally use the word 'adah in the meaning of 'urf; but the scholars of usul use both as synonyms.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal principle that custom ('urf) is a valid source for issuing fatwas, as long as it does not contradict explicit Shariah texts. The couplet by Allamah Shami states that custom holds weight in Shariah and many rulings are based upon it. The commentary explains that muftis must consider the prevalent customs of the people. Then, definitions of 'urf and 'adah are given: the first definition treats them synonymously as what is rationally settled and accepted by sound minds; the second distinguishes 'adah as mere repetition without rational basis, while 'urf is limited to actions that are rationally accepted. The key difference is that 'adah is broader (any repeated action), whereas 'urf is specifically those customs that are rational and socially approved. In fiqh, the terms are often used interchangeably by fuqaha, but usul scholars differentiate them.

Key Terms
عرف ('urf): What is settled in minds by reason and accepted by sound natures; a custom that is rationally acceptable.
عادت ('ādah): A repeated action without rational cause; broader than 'urf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) 'Urf is limited to what is rationally accepted, while 'ādah includes repetition without rational basis
C) 'Urf is based on analogy, while 'ādah is based on consensus
D) There is no difference; they are exact synonyms

Key Takeaway
Custom ('urf) is a valid basis for fatwa in Hanafi law, but only when it is rationally accepted and does not contradict explicit Shariah texts.

Change of Rulings Due to Changing Customs
Explains the principle that rulings change with custom and defines 'urf types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qa'idah 'al-'adah muhakkamah' to identify when a fatwa can be changed due to custom, and distinguish between 'urf 'amm and 'urf khas in Hanafi fatwa methodology.

Core Idea
Custom changes fatwas but cannot override clear texts.

The Hanafi school recognizes that legal rulings may evolve as customs change. This passage from Awn al-Mughni fi Hall 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti explains that many rulings of Imam Abu Hanifa were based on the customs of his time, and later jurists (mutakhkhirun) issued fatwas contrary to those rulings when customs changed—this is not opposition but adherence to the imam's methodology. The passage then defines two types of custom: general ('urf 'amm) and specific ('urf khas), each with its own scope and limitations. Understanding this distinction is essential for any mufti applying the qa'idah 'al-'adah muhakkamah'.

Guided Path
1
State the principle and its scope
Identify the main qa'idah: 'al-'adah muhakkamah' (custom is authoritative). Note that this principle is a major legal maxim (al-qawa'id al-kubra) applicable across all fiqh chapters, especially transactions and fatwa methodology.
The passage also mentions a related dabt: 'taghayyur al-ahkam bi taghayyur al-azman wa al-'awa'id' (rulings change with change of times and customs).
2
Distinguish between 'urf 'amm and 'urf khas
From the passage, list the definition of each: 'urf 'amm is prevalent in all countries; 'urf khas is limited to a particular city or region. Note that 'urf 'amm can specify qiyas and hadith, while 'urf khas cannot—it only establishes a specific ruling provided it does not contradict qiyas or hadith.
This distinction is crucial for determining the weight of custom in fatwa.
3
Analyze the istisna' example
Examine how general custom ('urf 'amm) makes the sale of istisna' permissible despite the general prohibition of selling non-existent objects. This is an example of takhsis (specification) of a text (the hadith forbidding bay' al-ma'dum) by 'urf 'amm.
The passage emphasizes that only istisna' is exempted; other sales of non-existent items remain prohibited, showing that custom does not completely abandon the text.
4
Determine the limitations of custom
Identify the istithna' (exceptions): custom cannot lead to abandoning or specifying a clear text (nass). For 'urf khas, it also cannot be used to specify qiyas or hadith. If it does, custom is invalid.
This ensures that definitive textual evidence (nass) always prevails over custom.
5
Apply the hierarchy to a new scenario
Using the principles, consider a modern custom (e.g., electronic contracts) and assess whether it qualifies as 'urf 'amm or 'urf khas, and whether it conflicts with any clear text. Then determine the ruling.
This step prepares you for real fatwa composition.

عرف عام کی مثال
استصناع ( آرڈر دے کر طبیع تیار کرانا) کی بیچ، یہ عرف و تعامل کی وجہ سے جائز ہے، حالاں کہ یہ غیر موجود شی ء کی بیع ہے، جو شرعاً ممنوع ہے ، مگر جس نص میں بیع معدوم سے منع کیا گیا ہے، اس میں تخصیص کرتے ہوئے ، بر بنائے تعامل صرف استصناع کی بیچ کو جائز قرار دیا گیا ہے، اس کے علاوہ میں بیچ معدوم کی ” نہی اپنی جگہ پر یہ دستور قائم ہے؛ لہذا نص کو بالکلیہ ترک کرنا لازم نہیں آیا۔ ہاں! اگر استصناع کے علاوہ بھی خرید وفروخت کی تمام صورتوں میں مبیع کے معدوم ہوتے ہوئے بھیج کو جائز قرار دیا جائے ، تو یہ نص کو ترک کرنا ہوگا ، جو کہ کسی بھی طرح درست نہیں۔

Awn al-Mughni fi Hall 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti
Change of rulings due to change of custom
104
Rulings change due to change of custom. There are many rulings that the founder of the school based on his own custom and the conditions of his time, but due to change of time or necessity they changed. For example: the later jurists giving fatwa of permissibility of taking wages for teaching Qur'an, leading prayer, teaching etc. due to necessity, and due to change of time and change of people's conditions, the apparent probity in witnesses not being sufficient, etc. Based on change of time or necessity etc., the later ones gave fatwa contrary to the statement of the imam of the school in many issues. Allama Shami in his Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti mentioned twenty-four such issues as examples in which the later jurists changed the fatwa based on the mentioned reasons. In reality, this change in fatwa is not opposition to the imam of the school, but rather agreement; because whatever opinion he formed in his time, he also formed it keeping in view the custom and habit and necessity of his time. So if this changing situation had come before him, based on the same reasons he would also change his opinion.
Custom is of two types
Further clarification about custom: custom has two types: • General custom • Specific custom
Awn al-Mughni fi Hall 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti
General custom: such a practice that is prevalent in all countries.
105
Specific custom: such a transaction that is prevalent in a particular city, not in the whole country. From general custom a general ruling is established, and through it specification is made in analogy (qiyas) and hadith.
The case of specific custom is different from that; because from it a specific ruling is established, on condition that it is not contrary to qiyas or hadith; because specific custom cannot be a specifier for qiyas and hadith.
Example of general custom
The sale of istisna' (order to manufacture) - this is permissible due to custom and practice, even though it is the sale of a non-existent thing, which is prohibited by Sharia. But while specifying the text that prohibits the sale of non-existent things, based on practice only the sale of istisna' is declared permissible. Apart from this, the prohibition of selling non-existent things remains in force; therefore it was not necessary to abandon the text entirely. Yes! If besides istisna' all forms of buying and selling are declared permissible even when the object of sale is non-existent, then that would be abandoning the text, which is not correct in any way.
If from specific custom there does not arise abandonment of text or specification of text, then specific custom is considered valid among its users in the conventional expressions concerning oaths (iman), and similarly in prevalent methods in sale transactions etc., even if it is contrary to the custom of Zahir al-Riwayah.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a rasm al-mufti rule concerning the change of legal rulings due to changing customs ('urf). It begins by stating the principle: rulings change with custom. Many rulings of the founder of the school (Abu Hanifa) were based on his own time's custom; later jurists (mutakhkhirin) changed fatwas in about twenty-four issues as listed by Allama Shami. This is not opposition but rather agreement, because the imam's ruling was also custom-dependent. The passage then defines two types of 'urf: 'urf 'amm (general custom) and 'urf khas (specific custom). General custom establishes a general ruling and can specify analogies (qiyas) and hadith. Specific custom establishes a specific ruling, but only if it does not contradict qiyas or hadith; it cannot serve as a specifier for those sources. The example of istisna' (manufacturing contract) illustrates general custom overriding the general prohibition of selling non-existent things, but only for that specific transaction, not all sales. Specific custom is valid in matters like oaths and sales, even if it contradicts the custom mentioned in Zahir al-Riwayah (the core transmitted texts), as long as it does not lead to abandoning or specifying a text.

Key Terms
عرف عام ('urf 'amm): A custom prevalent in all countries (universal in scope)
عرف خاص ('urf khas): A custom prevalent only in a particular city or region
استصناع (istisna'): A contract to manufacture a specific item (order to produce)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition is specific custom ('urf khas) valid in Hanafi fiqh?
A) It can never override any textual evidence
B) It is valid as long as it does not lead to abandoning or specifying a clear text (nass)
C) It is always valid because custom is authoritative
D) It is only valid if it is mentioned in Zahir al-Riwayah

Key Takeaway
Custom can change fatwas, but it cannot override clear textual evidence (nass) or be used to specify qiyas and hadith unless it is general ('urf 'amm).
